## Domain Knowledge
**Domain**: materials science & solid mechanics (thermo-mechanical constitutive modeling)
**Analysis**: The variables are stress (sigma), strain (epsilon), and temperature (T), which most naturally correspond to constitutive laws for solids (elasticity/plasticity/hyperelasticity) with temperature-dependent modulus, yield/flow stress, and thermal strain effects. Likely relationships include sigma increasing with epsilon (stiffening/hardening) while temperature modifies stiffness/strength or introduces a thermal-expansion strain offset.

**Common formula structures** (built-in generic + LLM domain-specific):
- Arrhenius equation: k = c0 * exp(c1 / T) (chemical reaction rate dependence on temperature)
- Coulomb's / Newton's inverse-square law: F = c0 / r**2 (gravitational or electrostatic force)
- Stefan-Boltzmann law: P = c0 * T**4 (blackbody radiation power vs temperature)
- Beer-Lambert law: I = c0 * exp(c1 * x) (light intensity attenuation through a medium)
- Michaelis-Menten kinetics: v = c0 * S / (S + c1) (enzyme-catalyzed reaction saturation)
- Hill equation: y = c0 * x**c1 / (x**c1 + c2) (cooperative binding, sigmoidal dose-response)
- Kepler's third law: T**2 = c0 * a**3 (orbital period vs semi-major axis)
- Newton's law of cooling: T = c0 + c1 * exp(c2 * t) (exponential thermal relaxation)
- Clausius-Clapeyron relation: log(P) = c0 + c1 / T (vapor pressure vs temperature)
- Langmuir adsorption isotherm: theta = c0 * P / (1 + c1 * P) (surface adsorption equilibrium)
- Freundlich isotherm: q = c0 * C**c1 (empirical adsorption on heterogeneous surface)
- Stokes' drag: F = c0 * r * v (viscous drag force on a sphere in fluid)
- Logistic growth: N = c0 / (1 + c1 * exp(c2 * t)) (population dynamics with carrying capacity)
- Planck-Wien approximation: I = c0 * f**3 * exp(c1 * f / T) (thermal radiation spectrum)
- Hooke’s law with temperature-dependent modulus: sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * epsilon (small-strain linear elasticity with E(T) approximated as linear in T)
- Thermoelasticity with thermal strain offset: sigma = c0 * (epsilon - c1 * (T - c2)) (stress from mechanical strain after subtracting thermal expansion strain)
- Separable thermo-mechanical scaling: sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * f(epsilon) (modulus/strength factor depends on T, shape depends on epsilon)
- Temperature-softened linear elasticity (exponential E(T)): sigma = c0 * exp(-c1 * T) * epsilon (rapid stiffness loss with temperature, e.g., polymers near softening)
- Power-law hardening with thermal factor: sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * epsilon**c2 (work hardening/ non-Hookean response with temperature-dependent strength)
- Offset power-law (Hollomon/Ludwik-type): sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * (epsilon + c2)**c3 (hardening with a pre-strain/offset; avoids singularity at epsilon=0)
- Polynomial nonlinearity (Landau-type expansion): sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * epsilon + (c2 + c3 * T) * epsilon**3 (weakly nonlinear elasticity with temperature-dependent coefficients)
- Exponential stiffening in strain: sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) * (exp(c2 * epsilon) - 1) (large-strain stiffening typical of some polymers/biomaterials)
- Voce-type saturation hardening with thermal dependence: sigma = (c0 + c1 * T) + (c2 + c3 * T) * (1 - exp(-c4 * epsilon)) (flow stress approaches saturation as strain accumulates)
- Bilinear elastic–plastic approximation: sigma = c0 * epsilon - c1 * Max(epsilon - c2, 0) (elastic slope then reduced tangent modulus after yield strain c2)
- Rubber-like (incompressible neo-Hookean, 1D stretch) with entropic scaling: sigma = c0 * T * ((1 + epsilon) - 1 / (1 + epsilon)**2) (entropy-driven stress proportional to absolute temperature)
- Glass-transition/phase-change softening via sigmoid in T: sigma = (c0 + c1 / (1 + exp(c2 * (T - c3)))) * epsilon (abrupt modulus drop around a transition temperature)
- Eyring/activated flow-style inverse-sinh response: sigma = c0 * T * asinh(c1 * epsilon) (rate-process inspired stress–strain shape with thermal scaling)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- If sigma is approximately linear in epsilon at small epsilon, use sigma ≈ E(T) * epsilon to represent elastic modulus and let E vary with T.
- A shift of the form (epsilon - c * (T - T0)) indicates thermal expansion: stress depends on mechanical strain relative to a temperature-dependent stress-free strain.
- Multiplicative separability sigma = f(epsilon) * g(T) often reflects independent mechanisms: strain hardening shape largely unchanged while temperature scales overall strength/stiffness.
- Power-law dependence in epsilon (epsilon**n) suggests plastic/work-hardening behavior; n>1 implies increasing tangent modulus with strain.
- Saturation forms like (1 - exp(-k * epsilon)) are characteristic of Voce-like hardening where dislocation density (or internal variables) approach a steady state.
- Strong large-strain stiffening is often captured better by exp(k*epsilon) or stretch-based forms than by low-order polynomials.
- Explicit proportionality to T (e.g., sigma ∝ T * g(epsilon)) is a hallmark of entropic elasticity (rubber-like response).
- A sigmoidal dependence on T typically indicates a transition region (e.g., glass transition) where modulus drops rapidly over a narrow temperature interval.
- asinh/log-like stress–strain shapes (e.g., sigma ∝ T * asinh(c*epsilon)) can indicate thermally activated deformation mechanisms (rate-process inspired constitutive forms).

I need to analyze each candidate against the Ground Truth (GT):

**GT:** `55.831574*epsilon*(-0.096961*(T - 325.31) + 1) + epsilon*6.648023*exp(-(T - 325.31)²)`

Let me expand the GT:
- First term: `55.831574*epsilon*(-0.096961*T + 0.096961*325.31 + 1)` = `55.831574*epsilon*(-0.096961*T + 32.539...) ` = `-5.414*epsilon*T + 55.831574*epsilon*(1 + 31.539...)` 

Wait, let me be more careful:
- `-0.096961*(T - 325.31) + 1 = -0.096961*T + 0.096961*325.31 + 1 = -0.096961*T + 31.539 + 1 = -0.096961*T + 32.539`

So GT = `55.831574*epsilon*(-0.096961*T + 32.539) + 6.648023*epsilon*exp(-(T-325.31)²)`

GT = `-5.414*T*epsilon + 1816.8*epsilon + 6.648*epsilon*exp(-(T-325.31)²)`

The GT has three terms:
1. **A*T*epsilon** (linear in T and epsilon)
2. **B*epsilon** (constant times epsilon)
3. **C*epsilon*exp(-(T-D)²)** (Gaussian in T times epsilon)

The Gaussian `exp(-(T-325.31)²)` is the critical term. Let me check which candidates can produce this.

Note: `exp(-(T-D)²) = exp(-T² + 2DT - D²) = exp(-D²)*exp(-T²)*exp(2DT)` — this is a Gaussian in T.

No simple combination of `cos`, `sin`, `log`, polynomial, or `exp(linear in T)` terms can reproduce a Gaussian `exp(-T²)`. So I need candidates that contain `exp(-(T-c)²)` or equivalent.

Let me scan all candidates for their basis functions:

Most candidates contain terms like:
- `cos(T*c)` — periodic, not Gaussian
- `exp(T*c)` — exponential, not Gaussian
- `exp(c/T)` — not Gaussian
- `sin(T*c + c)*cos(c*epsilon)` — periodic
- `cos(c*exp(T*c + c*epsilon))` — nested exponential in cosine
- `log(c*exp(T*c) + 1)` — softplus-like
- `epsilon/(c*exp(T*c + c) + 1)` — sigmoid-like
- `exp(c/epsilon²)` — not involving T as Gaussian

**None of these basis functions can produce a Gaussian in T**, i.e., `exp(-T²)` or `exp(-(T-c)²)`.

A Gaussian `exp(-(T-c)²)` is fundamentally different from:
- `exp(aT)` (exponential)
- `cos(bT)` (periodic)
- `log(...)` (logarithmic)
- Sigmoid functions
- Any polynomial

No candidate contains a term of the form `exp(-T²)` or `exp(-(T-c)²)`.

Let me verify each candidate systematically — every single one uses combinations of `exp(linear in T)`, `cos(T*c)`, `sin(T*c)`, `log(...)`, polynomials, or `exp(c/T)`. None has a quadratic exponent in T.

Therefore, **no candidate can match the GT** because the Gaussian term `exp(-(T-325.31)²)` cannot be represented by any of the basis functions present in any candidate.
